Business
The Year That Wasn't: Predictions Gone Wrong
By AnnaMaria Andriotis & Catey Hill
2912 words
24 December 2010
14:51
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2010 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.

A pick-up in consumer spending, healthy economic growth and falling unemployment resulting in modest stock gains -- that's what prominent economists predicted a year ago for 2010.

They had good reason. At the time, shoppers were slowly returning to stores. The unemployment rate was an alarming 10% but had fallen from an October peak of 10.1%. The gross domestic product surged in the fourth quarter of 2009 at an annualized rate of 5%. And history was on their side. Economic recoveries in 1983, 1994 and 2003 followed a pattern whereby spending begot zippy economic growth, and with it new jobs.

But this recovery has been anything but normal. U.S. stocks have returned a generous 15% this year, but shopping has picked up only a little (consumer spending is up 3%, just half a percentage point over 2009's gains). Recent economic growth -- an annualized 2.6% last quarter -- is at least half what was typical of past recoveries. The jobless rate remains high at 9.8%, even as the profits and cash stockpiles of corporations hit record levels during the year.

The persistently high unemployment rate raises fears that up to three million jobs in areas like manufacturing, construction and retail might never return, says Edward Leamer, director of the UCLA Anderson Forecast for economics. Missed 2010 forecasts and murky 2011 ones among experts could also be a sign that models are due for a change. "Most modeling has a one-size-fits-all attitude toward the economy," says Leamer.

Here are seven predictions where leading experts fell short in 2010 and their take on what's to come in 2011.

Next: Economic growth[http://www.smartmoney.com/investing/economy/2010-financial-and-economic-predictions-gone-wrong-1293085697172/?page=2]

Economic Growth

Expert: Dean Maki, chief U.S. economist, Barclay's Capital

Prediction: Maki predicted GDP would expand at a pace of 3.5% on an annualized basis.

Why: During the last quarter of 2009, GDP grew at 5%, similar to growth patterns after prior recessions. And economists had forecast an uptick in household spending and the worry of unemployment rates rocketing well over 10% had begun to ease. Typically, such factors play into continued GDP expansion and other economic indicators in late 2009 also pointed to a strong growth rate, says Maki.

What really happened and why: The inflation-adjusted GDP grew at an annualized rate of 2.6% through the third quarter of 2010; most analysts now expect the final 2010 GDP growth rate to be between 2.7% and 2.8%. The higher GDP growth Maki forecast didn't materialize partly because of several surprises in 2010, he says. Surging imports and declining exports led to "the largest drag on the economy from trade in more than 60 years," Maki says. What's more, productivity posted surprisingly large gains and "consumers [were] more cautious than we'd previously thought," he adds.

What Maki expects in 2011: He sees a "solid year" and predicts that the GDP will grow 3.1%. Maki sees consumer spending, pushed up by higher personal incomes and the lessening of housing woes, increasing by about 3.1%. He also believes that business investment spending, spurred by higher corporate earnings, will rise -- including a 14.8% jump in equipment and software spending and a 5.7% increase in spending on structures. Both will contribute to continued expansion of GDP, he says.

Next: Housing[http://www.smartmoney.com/investing/economy/2010-financial-and-economic-predictions-gone-wrong-1293085697172/?page=3]

Housing

Expert: Robert Shiller, professor of economics at Yale University and co-developer of the S&P/Case-Shiller index

Prediction: In December 2009, Shiller said, "I think that there is a likely scenario of a double dip in home prices." He believed that home prices would take a big fall again and that prices would stay in a "state of uncertainty" for the year.

Why: Shiller expected home sales to drop after the tax credit expired because he thought fewer consumers would be interested in buying a home once the free money disappeared. With fewer buyers and more homes on the market, home prices would decline once again. Shiller and his colleague, Dr. Karl Case, also expected home prices to sink after homeowners' adjustable-rate mortgages reset to higher rates; millions of loans were set to do so this year. That would have a domino effect: Fewer people would be able to afford bigger monthly payments and short sales and foreclosures would rise, sending home prices down.

What really happened: So far, that forecast double dip has been a small one. After home prices rose 4.7% between the first and second quarters of the year, they fell 2% in the third quarter, according to the S&P/Case-Shiller index. Rates on ARMs (and mortgages in general) fell to historic lows this year, in part because of the weak economy and a rush to the bond market. Adjusted rates on ARMS were actually lower than what borrowers were paying before. And with about 11 million homes underwater this year, more homeowners who had planned to sell have decided against putting homes on the market to avoid selling it at a low price, says Shiller. That's helped control the inventory for sale, which has kept home prices stronger than they otherwise would have been. And existing homes sales actually rose three of the last four months, which has helped stabilize prices (They're up 0.4% in November, year-over-year).

What Shiller expects in 2011: Shiller says he's no longer making forecasts. "I used to forecast home prices with some success because they would go in the same direction [for years], but there's been strange behavior since spring 2009," he says. Still, he doesn't believe home prices will fall at the same rates they did between 2006 and 2009. That said, Shiller says it's possible prices will fall another 5% between the third quarter of 2010 and third quarter of 2011. The Federal Reserve's second round of quantitative easing, which is designed to keep interest rates low, should help boost home sales and keep prices more stable, he says.

Next: Food Prices[http://www.smartmoney.com/investing/economy/2010-financial-and-economic-predictions-gone-wrong-1293085697172/?page=4]

Food Prices

Expert: Ephraim Leibtag, senior economist at the Agriculture Department's Economic Research Service

Prediction: In December 2009, Leibtag, who is also the USDA's food price forecaster, projected a 3% to 4% increase in food prices in 2010 because of rising consumer demand.

Why: Leibtag says the USDA expected higher consumer demand at grocery stores and in restaurants and also expected growing demand for food in general from developing countries. And with more corn and soybeans being used to make ethanol and biodiesel to power cars, the USDA projected prices would also continue to rise because of greater demand.

What really happened: Food prices are up 0.5% to 1.5% -- the lowest annual food inflation rate since 1992, according to the USDA Economic Research Service. The global spread of the economic slump led to weak consumer demand in the U.S. and abroad for American-grown staples like corn, wheat and soybeans. That actually led to a decline in food prices during the first two months of 2010. Consumer demand didn't move much in early 2010, so prices barely budged until September when they rose above the 1% mark and have remained steady since.

What Leibtag expects in 2011: He predicts an increase of 2% to 3% in food prices as consumer demand picks up slowly but steadily.

Next: Small Businesses[http://www.smartmoney.com/investing/economy/2010-financial-and-economic-predictions-gone-wrong-1293085697172/?page=5]

Small Business

Expert: Bill Dunkelberg, chief economist at the National Federation of Independent Business

Prediction: In October 2009, Dunkelberg said that as economic recovery seems more assured, small companies would want to borrow to beef up inventories and buy new equipment -- and that banks will be ready to help them then.

Why: The NFIB was expecting a recovery much like those that followed previous recessions when gross domestic product averaged 8% the year after the official end of a recession. Based on that projection, NFIB forecasted that small businesses would see an increase in consumer demand, followed by rising sales that would create incentives to borrow more money from banks to buy new equipments. And NFIB believed those same factors would prompt banks to open the lending spigot.

What really happened: The recession officially ended in June 2009, according to the National Bureau of Economic Research. But GDP didn't even crack 2% a year later. The slow recovery has meant lack of consumer demand, leaving little reason for small businesses to consider expansions. And while banks have increased lending this year somewhat, there are fewer small businesses who want to borrow. More than 50% of NFIB's members, made up of around 420,000 small businesses, say they're not interested in taking out loans, the highest number in 27 years. "You don't buy a new delivery truck if there are no new customers to deliver to," says Dunkelberg. Business owners who are looking for a loan need that money simply to stay afloat, but banks aren't giving loans to businesses with iffy balance sheets, he says.

What Dunkelberg expects in 2011: Small business inventory is starting to dwindle and as consumer spending picks up further – it's currently up 3% this year – more companies will look for ways to expand slowly, possibly buying equipment or hiring more people, predicts Dunkelberg on behalf of the NFIB. Still, because he expects the growth to be slow, he predicts small businesses won't get back to where they were on the loan front or the growth curve pre-recession even a year from now.

Next: Deflation and Inflation[http://www.smartmoney.com/investing/economy/2010-financial-and-economic-predictions-gone-wrong-1293085697172/?page=6]

Deflation and Inflation

Expert: Roubini Global Economics, chairman Nouriel Roubini, a.k.a. "Dr. Doom"

Prediction: In January, Roubini and his company RGE forecast a deflationary risk in part from falling demand for goods and a disinflationary trend in prices.

Why: Roubini and his company expected consumer spending to decline in 2010 and believed there would be an excess supply of labor and excess capacity in the manufacturing sector, says Prajakta Bhide, a research analyst at RGE (Roubini was unavailable for comment for this story). "Those three factors combined are deflationary," she says. What's more, U.S. consumers were scaling back on new debts, partly because of the wealth they lost when home values plummeted, she says.

What really happened and why: After two years of holding back, pent-up consumer demand gave way to purchases – especially for those who held on to their jobs through the recession and felt confident to spend again. By November, when the Federal Reserve rolled out a second round of quantitative easing, it further reduced the chances of deflation, says Bhide, since QE2 is mostly designed to boost aggregate demand by keeping borrowing costs low. The result has been overall inflation of 1% year-to-date on a seasonally adjusted annual rate, according to the Bureau of Labor Statistics, and core inflation (which excludes food and energy) of 0.6%.

What RGE expects next year: RGE expects overall inflation of around 1.4% in 2011 and core inflation of 1%. "Consumer spending [will] pick up more than we initially expected because of the payroll tax cut," which will leave people with more disposable income, says Bhide.

Next: Gold[http://www.smartmoney.com/investing/economy/2010-financial-and-economic-predictions-gone-wrong-1293085697172/?page=7]

Gold

Expert: Peter Schiff, president and chief global strategist of Euro Pacific Capital

Prediction: In early December 2009, when gold hit then-record-high prices above $1,200 an ounce, Schiff said that current gold prices were "not expensive" and that the prices would rise steadily in 2010 and for years after, eventually reaching $5,000 an ounce — possibly by the end of 2012.

Why: Schiff believed 2010 would bring sustained economic weakness, a buckling of the dollar and more quantitative easing — all of which would push gold prices higher as uneasy investors looked to hedge against currency devaluation.

What really happened and why: The price per ounce for gold has climbed more than 26% this year, hitting a record high of $1,431.25 an ounce on Dec. 7, and recently settling at about $1,380 an ounce. Demand for gold has been spurred by the debt crisis in Europe and the quantitative easing in the U.S., both of which harmed currencies. Gold is one of a number of commodities whose prices are on the rise: Silver is up more than 72%, coffee more than 65% and light crude oil about 21% — all as of Dec. 23.

What Schiff expects in 2011: "Gold will go a lot higher," he says. "It will outperform the U.S. stock market." Schiff expects more of the same economic issues, as weakening currencies around the globe, a slow-to-recover economy and continued quantitative easing encourage investors to go for gold. He still sees gold reaching $5,000 per ounce "over the next three to five years."

Next: Unemployment[http://www.smartmoney.com/investing/economy/2010-financial-and-economic-predictions-gone-wrong-1293085697172/?page=8]

Unemployment

Expert: David Wyss, chief economist at Standard & Poor's

Prediction: Near the end of 2009, Wyss said he believed unemployment would peak at about 10.4% in the second quarter of 2010 and then begin to fall.

Why: Wyss believed that steady economic growth -- he forecast that GDP would grow at an annual rate of 2.4% -- would spur job growth in mid-to-late 2010, leading to more hiring and a reduction in the unemployment rate. He projected the rate would top out at 10.4% because employment, a lagging indicator, usually takes months to begin growing even after the economy as a whole has improved.

What really happened and why: GDP has grown much as Wyss predicted (the revised third-quarter GDP released this week is 2.6% on an annualized basis), but the unemployment rate has remained stubbornly high and relatively stable, hovering between 9.5% and 9.9% in 2010. High rates of productivity, which Wyss had not expected, led to lower-than-expected employment growth, he says. "Productivity has been stronger throughout the recession than normal, in part because so much of the recession is concentrated in the low-productivity construction sector and in part because employers substituted equipment for workers in order to be competitive," he says. Add to that older workers who have delayed retirement and held on to their jobs and the result has been few job openings. Job creation has also remained stagnant after job losses continued through much of the year. And just 842,000 non-farm jobs were added to payrolls across the country, according to the Labor Department -- not nearly enough to make up for the more than eight million jobs lost during the recession.

What to expect in 2011: More of the same, he says. "We expect unemployment to average 9.5% for the year and close at about 9.25%," with most of that small decline happening in the second half of the year, he says. Wyss partly attributes these stagnant employment predictions to a "more cautious consumer" and stagnant demand, leading to less need to hire new workers. However, Wyss predicts the health-care sector will add between 40,000 and 50,000 jobs per month and that job creation in the lower-paying personal service sector — work in child care, fitness instruction, hair care and the like — will also grow, though at a slower rate.

Next: The Stock Market[http://www.smartmoney.com/investing/economy/2010-financial-and-economic-predictions-gone-wrong-1293085697172/?page=9]

The Stock Market

Expert: Harry Dent, founder and CEO of economic think tank HS Dent, author of "The Great Depression Ahead"

Prediction: In the summer of 2009, Dent said he believed that the U.S. was headed into a deeper recession and that stock prices would plummet throughout 2010 and 2011, with the Dow Jones Industrial Average bottoming out somewhere between 7,200 and 3,800 in mid-2012.

Why: Dent believed that the effects of the stimulus on the economy would wane, slowing economic growth. He also thought that baby boomers – important because of their sheer numbers and high incomes – would cut spending, further depressing the housing and retail markets. All of it would send the stock market reeling.

What really happened and why: Major indexes rose this year with the S&P 500 up about 13% year-to-date, Nasdaq up about 18% and the Dow rising about 11% (on Dec. 22, the Dow closed at 11,559). The markets were bolstered by reports of steady economic growth, solid corporate earnings and higher retail sales and consumer spending, among other factors. Major issues like the European debt crisis and a weak employment situation haven't left a lasting bruise thus far. Dent says the market decline he predicted didn't happen in 2010 because the effects of the stimulus lasted longer than expected and because the bond market has been slow to react to the threat of the mounting deficit.

What to expect in 2011: Dent still foresees the major market crash. "The markets will go up into March 2011 or so, but will then start to correct themselves," he says. The reason: "Deficit fears will play a big role." The U.S. government is trillions of dollars in debt and is facing high costs associated with an aging population — the first boomers turn 65 next year — who will soon demand Social Security and other benefits. Add to that reduced revenue as a result of tax cuts and Dent expects bond yields will rise and push stocks down, he says. He still believes "the Dow could hit 5000," in the next few years.

Credit Markets
Cheery Data Hit Treasurys
By Deborah Lynn Blumberg
518 words
24 December 2010
The Wall Street Journal
J
C7
English
(Copyright (c) 2010, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)

Treasurys posted a loss for the holiday-shortened week after some encouraging economic reports and as market participants readied for next week's final government note auctions of the year.

Thursday's trading ended the week, as financial markets are closed Friday for Christmas Eve.

Prices fell after a series of economic reports offered hope the economy will continue to recover heading into 2011. Weekly jobless claims fell more than expected, new home sales rose in November, and consumer sentiment pushed up in December.

Late in New York, the two-year note was off 2/32 point, or 62.5 cents for each $1,000 invested, to 99 22/32, to yield 0.663%, up from 0.630% late Wednesday, as prices and yields move inversely.

The 10-year fell 13/32 point, or $4.0625 for each $1,000 invested, to 93 19/32, to yield 3.392%, from 3.342% late Wednesday.

Losses left the bond market in better shape heading into the coming week's Treasury auctions, as lower prices could help lure more buyers.

Market participants prepared for the sales by driving prices down in order to get a better price at the auctions, known as building in a concession. Some say the auctions could draw somewhat lackluster demand as trading desks typically are thinly staffed in the year's final week.

Each of the past six late-December two-year auctions have tailed, noted David Ader, head of government bond strategy at CRT Capital Group, meaning that the yield on the new notes was higher than that on the when-issued notes just before the sale.

Treasury Sets Sales

The Treasury will sell $156 billion in bills and notes next week. Details of the offerings (all with minimum denominations of $100):

-- Monday: $29 billion in three-month bills and $28 billion in six-month bills will be sold.

The bills will be dated Dec. 30 and mature March 31, 2011, and June 30, 2011, respectively. The Cusip number for the three-month bills is 9127952J9 and for the six-month bills is 912795X22.

Noncompetitive tenders for the bills must be received by 11 a.m. EST Monday, and competitive tenders by 11:30 a.m.

Also Monday, $35 billion in two-year notes will be sold, dated Dec. 31, 2010, and maturing Dec. 31, 2012.

The Cusip number is 912828PW4. Noncompetitive tenders must be received by noon Monday and competitive tenders by 1 p.m.

-- Tuesday: $35 billion in five-year notes will be sold, dated Dec. 31, 2010, and maturing Dec. 31, 2015. The Cusip number is 912828PM6. Noncompetitive tenders must be received by noon Tuesday, and competitive tenders by 1 p.m. (Details on the usual four-week bill sale will be announced Tuesday.)

-- Wednesday: $29 billion in seven-year notes will be sold, dated Dec. 31, 2010, and maturing Dec. 31, 2017. The Cusip number is 912828PN4. Noncompetitive tenders must be received by noon Wednesday, and competitive tenders by 1 p.m.

U.S. News -- Remembrances: Real Estate Deal Maker Gave His Hedge Fund A Leg Up
By Lingling Wei
578 words
24 December 2010
The Wall Street Journal
J
A5
English
(Copyright (c) 2010, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)

Keith F. Barket 1961-2010

Keith F. Barket, the global head of Angelo, Gordon & Co.'s real-estate group, came from a family of property investors and was quick to recognize the bargains that abounded in the wake of the early 1990s recession.

Angelo Gordon was his partner on many of those deals. One day, the investment firm called him up and offered him a job.

"He truly had it all -- an analyst, a mentor and a leader," said John Angelo, co-founder and chief executive of the New York-based firm.

Mr. Barket, who died Wednesday at age 49 of peritoneal mesothelioma, a rare form of cancer of the abdominal lining, vaulted Angelo Gordon, one of the world's largest hedge-fund firms, into the top league of private-equity real-estate investment.

Angelo Gordon now has more than 50 professionals working in its real-estate department. During the past 17 years, the firm has invested more than $5 billion of equity in about 300 deals, and Mr. Barket has helped it build a major presence in China.

A St. Louis native, he was known for his sanguine personality and down-to-earth manners in real-estate and private-equity circles. "You always knew where you stood with him," said hotelier Ian Schrager. "It's a breath of fresh air in our business."

During the more recent real-estate downturn, many fund managers and property developers have been struggling with heavily indebted deals made at the top of the market. But Mr. Barket's team emerged relatively unscathed and has become an active buyer of distressed real estate.

In the past 18 months, Angelo Gordon has invested more than $1 billion of equity in about 50 deals, including the high-profile purchase of the Helmsley Carlton House hotel and apartment building in Manhattan. In addition, the firm is one of the few private-equity fund managers that has successfully raised money. It recently closed a $625 million fund to invest in Asia's property markets.

Both Mr. Barket's father and brother have been active in real-estate investment. A graduate of Georgetown University, he got his M.B.A. from the Wharton School of the University of Pennsylvania and was active during the 1990s doing deals with the Resolution Trust Corp., the government agency set up to liquidate assets, mostly real estate, seized from failed thrifts.

Mr. Barket joined Angelo Gordon in 1997 and did his first major deal one year later amid the market turbulence caused by the Russia debt crisis: the $60 million purchase of the Chelsea Market complex in Manhattan. The firm converted the buildings from manufacturing to office and retail. It has attracted tenants such as the Food Network and Oxygen Network, and today, by some estimates, is valued at more than $850 million.

"Keith saw the fantastic long-term opportunities in the property," recalled Adam Schwartz, who heads the firm's U.S. real-estate investment.

Mr. Barket was an avid tennis player and was proud of his partial Lebanese heritage, his family and friends said. "His life was a life of celebration," said his younger brother, Sol. "He was a realist, and he was always the one who would say, 'Cut your losses and move on with life.' "

Remembrances

U.S. News: Economic Growth Exceeds Forecasts
By Conor Dougherty
497 words
23 December 2010
The Wall Street Journal
J
A4
English
(Copyright (c) 2010, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)

The economy grew slightly faster than the government previously estimated in the third quarter, and forecasters have been raising growth projections for the fourth quarter and for 2011.

Optimism about the U.S. recovery has grown sharply in the past month, fueled by a series of economic reports showing everything from consumers spending more freely to businesses curbing layoffs.

The nation's gross domestic product, a broad measure of goods and services produced by the economy, rose 2.6% in the third quarter, compared with the 2.5% growth the government previously estimated, the Commerce Department said Wednesday. This followed 1.7% growth in the second quarter.

The report showed exports were strong and businesses spent more than previously thought on restocking shelves in the third quarter. That could indicate they expect stronger sales ahead.

However, consumer spending was lower in the period than the government previously estimated.

The economy has shown signs of accelerating during the fourth quarter, and economists are predicting a strong holiday shopping season. The revival of consumer spending is also expected to continue next year, thanks in part to the large package of federal tax cuts signed into law earlier this month.

The sunnier outlook has led many economists to boost U.S. growth estimates for the fourth quarter and for 2011. Over recent weeks, eight banks and private economic-research firms surveyed by The Wall Street Journal, including J.P. Morgan Chase & Co., Goldman Sachs Group Inc. and Macroeconomic Advisers, have raised their growth estimates for the last three months of the year to an average of 3.5% from 2.6% earlier in the month.

"Bottom line: People are less scared about a double dip," said Nariman Behravesh, chief economist at forecasting firm IHS Global Insight. Mr. Behravesh's firm recently increased its fourth-quarter forecast to 3.5% from 3.0% earlier in December.

Separately, the National Association of Realtors on Wednesday reported that U.S. sales of existing homes rose 5.6% in November from a month earlier, to a seasonally adjusted annual rate of 4.68 million units. Sales of used homes represent more than 90% of house sales in the U.S. The average price for an existing home was up for the first time since August, to $170,600 in November. That was an increase of 0.4% from a year ago.

The housing market is still extremely weak, despite the steadily improving outlook elsewhere in the economy. There is a huge stock of unsold homes, and roughly a third of existing homes sold were under some sort of financial stress, either in the form of foreclosure or homes sold for less than the owner owed the bank.

The inventory of previously owned homes listed for sale fell 4.0% at the end of November to 3.71 million units.

Credit Markets
Treasury Prices Fall for Week; Auctions Ahead
By Deborah Lynn Blumberg
524 words
23 December 2010
17:38
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2010 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.

Treasurys posted a loss for the holiday-shortened week after some encouraging economic reports and as market participants readied for next week's final government note auctions of the year.

Thursday's trading ended the week, as financial markets are closed Friday for Christmas Eve.

Prices fell after a series of economic reports offered hope the economy will continue to recover heading into 2011. Weekly jobless claims fell more than expected, new home sales rose in November, and consumer sentiment pushed up in December.

Late in New York, the two-year note was off 2/32 point, or 62.5 cents for each $1,000 invested, to 99 22/32, to yield 0.663%, up from 0.630% late Wednesday, as prices and yields move inversely.

The 10-year fell 13/32 point, or $4.0625 for each $1,000 invested, to 93 19/32, to yield 3.392%, from 3.342% late Wednesday.

Losses left the bond market in better shape heading into the coming week's Treasury auctions, as lower prices could help lure more buyers.

Market participants prepared for the sales by driving prices down in order to get a better price at the auctions, known as building in a concession. Some say the auctions could draw somewhat lackluster demand as trading desks typically are thinly staffed in the year's final week.

Each of the past six late-December two-year auctions have tailed, noted David Ader, head of government bond strategy at CRT Capital Group, meaning that the yield on the new notes was higher than that on the when-issued notes just before the sale.

Treasury Sets Sales

The Treasury will sell $156 billion in bills and notes next week. Details of the offerings (all with minimum denominations of $100):

• Monday: $29 billion in three-month bills and $28 billion in six-month bills will be sold.

The bills will be dated Dec. 30 and mature March 31, 2011, and June 30, 2011, respectively. The Cusip number for the three-month bills is 9127952J9 and for the six-month bills is 912795X22.

Noncompetitive tenders for the bills must be received by 11 a.m. EST Monday, and competitive tenders by 11:30 a.m.

Also Monday, $35 billion in two-year notes will be sold, dated Dec. 31, 2010, and maturing Dec. 31, 2012.

The Cusip number is 912828PW4. Noncompetitive tenders must be received by noon Monday and competitive tenders by 1 p.m.

• Tuesday: $35 billion in five-year notes will be sold, dated Dec. 31, 2010, and maturing Dec. 31, 2015. The Cusip number is 912828PM6. Noncompetitive tenders must be received by noon Tuesday, and competitive tenders by 1 p.m. (Details on the usual four-week bill sale will be announced Tuesday.)

• Wednesday: $29 billion in seven-year notes will be sold, dated Dec. 31, 2010, and maturing Dec. 31, 2017. The Cusip number is 912828PN4. Noncompetitive tenders must be received by noon Wednesday, and competitive tenders by 1 p.m.

ARM's surge leads European stocks

European stocks extended their winning streak to three sessions Wednesday, boosted by car firms and chip maker ARM Holdings.

The Stoxx Europe 600 index rose 0.1% to 281.45, its highest since September 2008. It is up 11.2% year to date.

"A positive [factor] that is lifting Europe is coming from the U.S. where forecasters are revising up their GDP forecasts and some of them are even going for 4% in 2011," said Stephen Pope, managing partner at Spotlight Ideas.

U.S. third-quarter gross domestic product was revised up slightly to 2.6% from a prior estimate of 2.5%, while data from the National Association of Realtors showed sales of existing U.S. homes rose 5.6% in November.

While the reports pointed to a still-recovering U.S. economy, bank stocks benefitted from recent merger activity, helping push stocks to two-year highs. In late trade, the Dow Jones Industrial Average was up 21.64 points, or 0.2%, at 11554.80. The Nasdaq Composite was up 0.2% at 2671.62and the Standard & Poor's 500 index advanced 0.3% to 1258.36.

Treasurys prices dropped as the Federal Reserve completed its last debt purchase of the week, buying $2.5 billion in Treasurys maturing in the next 11 to 17 years. The benchmark 10-year note fell 5/32 in price to yield 3.342%.

Autos supported the gains in Europe, as reflected in a 1.3% gain for Renault and a 0.5% rise for Volkswagen. Mr. Pope pointed to a new Gallup survey that referred to optimism for 2011 in emerging markets, notably in Brazil, India and China. "As the middle classes expand, they will want more autos and better health care, so profit potential is being priced in," he said.

In London, the FTSE 100 index rose 0.5% to 5983.49, supported by a 9.1% rally in ARM Holdings. The Wall Street Journal said Microsoftwill announce a new version of its Windows operating system that runs on technology provided by ARM.

In Paris, the CAC 40 indexended off 0.2% at 3919.71, but shares of LVMH Moet Hennessy Louis Vuitton rose 1.8%. Late Tuesday, the company said it was raising to 20% its stake in Hermes International. Hermes gained 2.3%.

In Frankfurt, the DAX 30 index fell 0.1% to 7067.92. Infineon Technologies rose 1.6%.

Signs of stabilization in the struggling U.S. housing market helped the dollar gain against the euro. Late afternoon in New York, the euro was at $1.3088, down from $1.3096 late Tuesday. The euro fell as low as 1.2440 francs and the dollar fell to its lowest against the franc since its record low in October.

Crude-oil futures climbed 45 cents to $90.27 a barrel on the New York Mercantile Exchange, following a deeper-than-expected drop in crude-oil stockpiles last week.

Comex gold futures fell $1.40, or 0.1%, to $1,386 an ounce as China's support for Europe's troubled debt markets tempered haven buying.

In other major stock market action:

British Airways rose 1.9%, recovering some ground lost as harsh winter weather disrupted flights across Europe in recent days.

Suez Environnement in Paris and Severn Trent in London each rose 2.1% as utility stocks firmed.

Economic growth in U.S. shows signs of picking up
By Conor Dougherty
540 words
23 December 2010
The Wall Street Journal Europe
WSJE
1
English
(Copyright (c) 2010, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)

The U.S. economy grew slightly faster than the government previously estimated in the third quarter, and forecasters are raising growth projections for the fourth quarter and for 2011.

The nation's gross domestic product, a broad measure of goods and services produced by the economy, increased 2.6% in the third quarter, the Commerce Department said Wednesday.

In the government's previous report on how the economy performed, released a month ago, GDP was estimated to have risen 2.5% in the third quarter. This followed 1.7% growth in the second quarter.

Optimism for the U.S. recovery has grown sharply in the past month, fueled by a series of economic reports showing everything from consumers spending more freely to businesses curbing layoffs.

Wednesday's report showed exports strong and businesses spending more than previously thought on restocking shelves in the third quarter, a good sign since it can indicate they expect stronger sales ahead.

However, consumer spending was lower in the period than the government previously estimated.

The economy has shown signs of accelerating as it moved into the fourth quarter, and economists are predicting a strong holiday shopping season. The revival of consumer spending is also expected to continue next year, thanks in part to the large package of federal tax cuts signed into law earlier this month.

The sunnier outlook has led many economists to boost U.S. growth estimates for the fourth quarter and for 2011. Over recent weeks, eight banks and private economic research firms surveyed by The Wall Street Journal, including J.P. Morgan Chase & Co., Goldman Sachs Group Inc. and Macroeconomic Advisers, have raised their growth estimates for the last three months of the year to an average of 3.5% from 2.6% earlier in the month.

"Bottom line: People are less scared about a double dip," said Nariman Behravesh, chief economist at forecasting firm IHS Global Insight. Mr. Behravesh's firm recently increased its fourth-quarter forecast to 3.5% from 3.0% earlier in December.

Separately, the National Association of Realtors on Wednesday reported that U.S. sales of existing homes rose 5.6% in November from a month earlier, to a seasonally adjusted annual rate of 4.68 million units. Sales of used homes represent more than 90% of house sales in the U.S. The average price for an existing home was up for the first time since August, to $170,600 in November. That was an increase of 0.4% from a year ago.

The housing market is still extremely weak, despite the steadily improving outlook elsewhere in the economy. There is a huge stock of unsold homes, and roughly a third of existing homes sold were under some sort of financial stress, either in the form of foreclosure or homes sold for less than the owner owed the bank.

The inventory of previously owned homes listed for sale fell 4.0% at the end of November to 3.71 million units. That's a 9.5-month supply at the current sales pace, compared with a 10.5-month supply in October.

The Property Report
Deal of the Week: Back to Basics for Tishman
By Maura Webber Sadovi
757 words
22 December 2010
The Wall Street Journal
J
C6
English
(Copyright (c) 2010, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)

Tishman Speyer Properties' purchase of a new Chicago office tower is the latest sign that the firm is going back to the basics as it emerges from one of the most devastating commercial real-estate cycles in decades.

Its purchase of 353 North Clark St. for $385 million, an estimated $55 million less than it cost to build, also exposes the fault lines as well as the opportunities for investors and owners as the market recovers.

Tishman Speyer built its reputation in the office market. But during the boom years it played a leading role in the ill-fated acquisitions of multifamily company Archstone-Smith Trust and Manhattan's Stuyvesant Town and Peter Cooper Village apartment complex.

Those deals and a few others collapsed, tarnishing the firm's image. Now, as it puts its problems behind it, Tishman Speyer is cranking up its deal engine focusing on the types of acquisitions it knows best.

Besides 353 North Clark, Tishman also announced this week that it bought a 327,000-square-foot office building along the Seine River in a suburb of Paris for about 100 million euros ($131 million). It also said it is buying back a majority stake in and regaining sole ownership of a three-building Silicon Valley office complex in California where Juniper Networks has its headquarters.

These purchases make it clear that Tishman Speyer will continue to be a major player, despite its problems in recent years. Tishman Speyer, which has invested in more than $50 billion of property since 1978, owns more than 50 million square feet globally.

Also, any losses the firm suffered were shared among investors. Principals Jerry Speyer and son Rob Speyer also put some of their own money into deals. But, like most successful developers, they attracted numerous individuals and institutions eager to invest alongside them.

The Speyers, who declined to comment, have said in the past that their firm has made more than 20% annual returns on investments since its inception three decades ago.

Some of these investors and others are sticking with Tishman Speyer. The firm has more than $2 billion available from its prerecession fund-raising efforts. It also has succeeded in raising fresh money, particularly to shore up some of its troubled office-building deals, like its purchase in 2006 of the CarrAmerica portfolio in Washington, D.C., from private-equity firm Blackstone Group LP in a deal valued at $2.8 billion

"As long as investment managers are honest and upfront about what went wrong, their investors tend to be understanding," said Greg MacKinnon, director of research for the Pension Real Estate Association.

The value of Tishman Speyer's property acquisitions globally has risen from about $99 million in 2009 to about $1.06 billion this year. The dollar volume of its sales also has increased from $500 million last year to about $1.9 billion this year.

The Chicago deal shows that there is plenty of opportunity out there. The developer of the 1.2-million-square-foot tower, a venture led by Mesirow Financial, was under pressure from about $374 million in debt coming due last month, including a construction loan held by Hypo Real Estate Holding AG and a mezzanine loan of about $44 million, according to a person familiar with the transaction. Mesirow, which was represented by Jones Lang LaSalle in the deal, declined to comment.

Tishman Speyer also benefited from the return of the commercial mortgage-backed securities market as a debt financing source. It will provide about 50% to 60% of the purchase price in the 353 North Clark deal, according to a person with knowledge of the deal.

The building's annual net income of about $21 million represents about a 5.5% return on the purchase price. Tishman Speyer can boost that return because the building is only 79% leased.

But there are market risks, too. Chicago's office-vacancy rate rose to 16% in the third quarter from 15.5% in the year-earlier period, and average asking rents fell to $22.55 a square foot from $22.92, according to CoStar Group Inc. The River North submarket where the building is located has seen its vacancy rate rise to 18.1% from 8.2% in the fourth quarter of 2008 as it still is struggling to absorb new space, including some of the empty floors in 353 North Clark.

Dow Jones & Company, Inc.

Economy
Existing Home Sales Rise
By Tom Barkley And Jeff Bater
503 words
22 December 2010
13:11
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2010 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.

Existing-home sales rose less than expected last month, though prices stabilized as fewer houses remained on the market.

Demand for used homes increased by 5.6% to a seasonally adjusted annual rate of 4.68 million, the National Association of Realtors said Wednesday.

Economists surveyed by Dow Jones Newswires had expected home sales in November to increase by 6.5%, to an annual rate of 4.72 million.

Still, the price for an existing home edged up for the first time since August, to $170,600 in November. That's up 0.4% from the year-ago median price of $170,000 and an improvement from a downwardly revised $170,400 in October.

A high level of unsold homes has contributed to the recent drop in prices. The inventory of previously owned homes listed for sale shrunk by 4.0% at the end of November to 3.71 million available for sale. That represented a 9.5-month supply at the current sales pace, compared with a 10.5-month supply in October. But a big supply of unlisted bank-owned homes and potential foreclosures loom on the horizon, a "shadow inventory" that could push down prices.

The housing market is a sore spot within the economy, which is growing slowly out of the recession.

Economists predict the housing sector has a better chance of recovering when the job market improves. Rising unemployment has scared many consumers away from the market from buying a house. And while mortgage rates are low, some people have had difficulty getting a loan to make a purchase.

A healthy level of home sales would be an annualized rate of just over 5 million home sales. The NAR predicts about 4.8 million homes will be sold this year.

Lawrence Yun, the chief economist at NAR, said he expects sales to recover to around 5.2 million next year, which would be in line with "more sustainable levels" last seen in 2000.

Given that seasonal factors contributed to last month's inventory reduction, the number of homes on the market is expected to climb back up to around 4 million by spring, said Yun. Still, a sales rate of around 5.2 million should create a "stable home-value situation next year," he said.

Sales of previously occupied homes in October declined by an unrevised 2.2% to 4.43 million. For the past year, resales were down 27.9% since November 2009, when the government was providing tax credits to support the market.

A new problem for the housing market emerged in the fall, with revelations that several banks made errors on court documents used to evict homeowners. Some buyers avoided foreclosures because of the document crisis, hurting sales.

Foreclosures have climbed because Americans out of work couldn't keep up with payments on mortgages taken out during the housing boom. The NAR said distressed properties accounted for about a third of sales in November.

Dow Jones & Company, Inc.

Economy
Jobs and Housing Markets Perk Up
By Sudeep Reddy
466 words
17 December 2010
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2010 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.

New jobless claims fell again last week, providing the latest sign that the labor market may be picking up, while an uptick in housing starts offered a modest boost for a troubled sector of the economy.

Initial claims for unemployment benefits declined 3,000 to 420,000 in the week ending December 11, the Labor Department said Thursday. The four-week moving average of claims, a measure used to smooth weekly volatility in the data, fell 5,250 to 422,750—the sixth straight weekly decline. Continuing claims, reflecting benefits drawn for more than a week, rose 22,000 to 4.14 million in the week ending December 4.

New jobless claims are down to their lowest level since the summer 2008, before the financial crisis sent layoffs surging. The figures suggest the government's report on December employment—due early next month—will be better than the disappointing November report, which said payrolls increased by just 39,000 last month.

The latest report on jobless claims "strengthens our expectation that the November payrolls figure will be revised higher or that the December reading will show a nice gain that corroborates other statistical and anecdotal evidence showing a healthier labor market," RBS economists said in a note to clients.

Meanwhile, new housing starts rose 3.9% in November to a seasonally adjusted annual rate of 555,000, the Commerce Department said. The gain was driven by a 6.9% increase in new construction of single-family homes, which represent 80% of all starts, to a six-month high. Multifamily construction, a traditionally volatile measure, declined.

The figures were only a modest positive in a sector that's struggling with a glut of supply. More existing homes are expected to hit the market in the next year as lenders foreclose on overdue borrowers, putting downward pressure on overall house prices.

The home-construction market is unlikely to recover significantly anytime soon given the high inventory on the market. Housing starts should be at a rate of about 900,000 given today's pace of population growth and the current replacement rate for homes, said economist Paul Dales of Capital Economics. "That shows just how much weak demand and the current high supply is weighing on home-building," he said.

Building permits, a sign of future construction, fell 4% in November to an annual pace of 530,000. But that was driven by the volatile multifamily-housing segment; permits for single-family homes rose modestly during the month.

Housing starts showed their best performance in the Midwest, rising almost 16%. They rose about 2% in the South and the West. But starts fell 2.5% in the Northeast.

Dow Jones & Company, Inc.

FRIDAY JOURNAL --- The Home Front: Private Properties
By Juliet Chung and Candace Jackson
494 words
17 December 2010
The Wall Street Journal
J
D10
English
(Copyright (c) 2010, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)

Ringo Starr Lists

Home Near Aspen

Ringo Starr and his wife, Barbara, are asking $4.5 million for their three-bedroom vacation home near Aspen, Colo.

The 70-year-old Beatles drummer, who continues to tour with his All Starr Band, bought the home in 1990. The wood cabin sits on about 16 flat acres in Woody Creek and has frontage on Roaring Fork River. The roughly 3,200-square-foot cabin was built in 1987 and is surrounded by unused horse paddocks.

"It's like a ranch-ette on the Roaring Fork," says Joshua Saslove of Joshua & Co., a Christie's Great Estates affiliate, who represents the sellers. He said his clients have been using the home less frequently in the last few years. Hunter S. Thompson and musician Don Henley have had homes in Woody Creek.

Lackey of Red Sox Buys in Boston

Red Sox pitcher John Lackey bought a penthouse in Boston's Back Bay for just-under $2.1 million, 13% below its original, nearly $2.4 million asking price in June.

The 32-year-old starter, who signed a five-year, $82.5 million deal with the Sox last December, bought a three-bedroom condominium of nearly 2,400 square feet in a townhouse. The living room looks onto the Charles River and Cambridge, and there's a terrace with views of Back Bay. The master bedroom suite has vaulted ceilings and a walk-in closet, and there's also a formal dining room. Tracy Campion of Campion & Co. represented Mr. Lackey. Rick Egan of Coldwell Banker represented the sellers.

L.A. Home of Art World's Brodys

Sells for $14.9 Million

The Los Angeles home of late art collectors Frances and Sidney F. Brody has sold for $14.9 million. It originally listed last spring for $25 million and most recently asked $19.5 million.

The A. Quincy Jones-designed modernist style house was where the Brodys displayed their extensive art collection, including a Picasso painting that sold at a Christie's auction in May for $106.5 million, a record for the most expensive work of art ever sold at auction.

The 11,500-square-foot home, located on 2.3 acres in the Holmby Hills neighborhood, has five bedrooms and five bathrooms, with floor-to-ceiling glass windows as well as a guest house. There's also a swimming pool and four additional staff bedrooms in the main house.

Mrs. Brody, who lived in the home for nearly 60 years, died last year at age 93.

Drew Fenton of Hilton & Hyland represented the buyer, whom he declined to identify but described as a local real-estate investor who plans to renovate the house. Linda May and Jade Mills of Coldwell Banker Previews International had the listing.

New York Real Estate
More at Home in 4 Bedrooms; City Developers Add to Supply of Larger Apartments as Demand for Space Grows
By Melanie Lefkowitz
866 words
14 December 2010
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2010 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.

(Please see Corrections & Amplifications item below[mark] .)

After Louise Phillips Forbes had her second child, she didn't even consider moving to the suburbs. Instead, she sought a four-bedroom apartment in Manhattan.

"I joke around with my husband that we had a prenup that said that we were raising our kids in the city," says Ms. Forbes, an executive vice president of Halstead Property. "For me, the ability to have a play date in a vertical environment, and not getting in my car and driving somewhere, is phenomenal."

The number of families living in the city has been rising in recent years as has the number of five-person families, according to U.S. Census estimates and state birth-rate data. Those factors, combined with a flood of larger apartments conceived at the height of the real-estate boom, have led to an increase in urban apartments with suburban proportions.

The number of four-bedroom apartments sold in Manhattan has nearly doubled over the past five years, to 270 in 2009 from 136 in 2005, according to the appraisal firm Miller Samuel Inc. Sales of large apartments began rising in the 1990s, only to decline sharply after the economic crash of 2001. But in the past five years they have resumed their upward trend, with sales of apartments with four or more bedrooms rising by 29% in the third quarter from the second quarter.

For many years, Manhattan buyers seeking four-bedroom apartments were largely limited to a handful of prewar buildings or they faced the often tricky process of construction to combine adjacent units. Ms. Forbes, for example, put together two apartments about four years ago.

But more new high-end buildings—especially those in such family oriented neighborhoods as the Upper West and Upper East Sides—have included a higher proportion of large apartments, and developers and brokers say that those apartments are selling well.

"I believe what we're seeing is, the demographics of New York City over the last five years have really changed significantly, where you see a lot of young families electing to stay in Manhattan and not move to suburbia," said Susan de Franca, president of Related Sales, a unit of developer Related Cos. "There's a significant demand for family-sized apartments."

Starting around 2004, Ms. de Franca says, her company began planning its new developments with these larger families in mind. Related's Brompton, on the Upper East Side, includes 27 apartments with four or more bedrooms, and the Harrison, which is on the Upper West Side and includes 16 apartments with four or more bedrooms. Both are "virtually sold out," she says.

Traditionally, new developments consisted mainly of studios and one- and two-bedroom apartments, brokers and developers say. Because they were priced lower, these apartments appeal to a larger segment of buyers and sold more quickly than larger units, so they are considered less of a gamble. But for some developers, that conventional wisdom is changing.

"In the locations where we were building, we felt it wasn't that big a risk," says Gary Barnett, president of Extell Development Co. "We actually have deliberately built quite a few larger-sized apartments, and we have absolutely seen a very strong demand for them."

Over the past few months, he says, several four bedrooms—and even some five and six-bedrooms—have sold at the Lucida, a condominium tower at East 85th Street and Lexington Avenue. Large apartments also have sold at 535 West End and at the Rushmore, at 80 Riverside Blvd. Prices for the units have ranged between $3.5 million and $17 million, he says.

According to the U.S. Census's New York City Housing and Vacancy Survey, apartments with four or more bedrooms represented more than 12% of owner-occupied units in New York City in 2008, compared with 12.8% in 2005. The city's Buildings Department doesn't track new units by the number of bedrooms.

Because they remain relatively scarce, four-bedroom apartments cost more per square foot than three-bedroom units, which, in turn, cost more than two-bedroom apartments; prices for these large units can climb to $20 million or more.

The median price per square foot for Manhattan four-bedroom apartments in new developments currently on the market is $1,793, 11% higher than the median price per square foot of three-bedrooms and 46% higher than two-bedrooms, according to StreetEasy.com.

"If you can afford it, it's much more gracious to be able to have a larger space," says Extell's Mr. Barnett. "It's a nicer way of living."

Corrections & Amplifications According to the U.S. Census' New York City Housing and Vacancy Survey, apartments with four or more bedrooms represented more than 12% of owner-occupied units in New York City in 2008, compared with 12.8% in 2005. This article incorrectly said that apartments with four or more bedrooms accounted for 1% of the owner-occupied units in the city in 2005.

New York Real Estate
Bargain Price Comes With Challenges; Private-Equity Firm Acquires Office Building for $135 Million; Next Step, Upgrades to Facade, Lobby, Entrance and More
By Craig Karmin
615 words
13 December 2010
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2010 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.

A private-equity firm that has been focusing on distressed and unusual New York properties bought a Manhattan office building for $135 million in a sign that there are still bargains in the city's commercial real estate market for investors who are bullish on the city's future.

The price Savanna paid for the 513,000-square-foot property at 1375 Broadway came to $263 a square foot. That's one of the lowest prices paid this year for a New York office building.

But the building in the Garment District also comes with a number of challenges including the need of upgrades to its facade, lobby, common corridors and entrance. Savanna expects to spend about $20 million over five years on those improvements and leasing costs, though some industry experts think the cost could run higher.

The property at 37th Street, which is about 90% leased, also doesn't currently chalk up great financial performance. Its annual net income represents roughly a 3.8% return on the $135 million that Savanna is paying. Buyers of other office property this year have been looking at returns—often referred to in the real estate industry as "capitalization rates"—in the 5% to 6% range.

The building appealed to Savanna partly because about half of its leases are up over the next five years. The building's average rent is about $20 a square foot while comparable properties south of Times Square on Broadway leases begin around the high $30s a square foot range, according to Nicholas Bienstock, a Savanna managing partner. So he expects to be able to increase rent even if the market rate stays flat.

The deal comes as investors throughout the world have been bidding up the price of modern Manhattan office buildings, especially those in prime locations. For example, last summer Mortimer Zuckerman's Boston Properties paid close to an estimated $1,000 a square foot for a new, but empty 30-story office tower on Madison Ave. and 53rd Street.

The purchase of 1375 Broadway marks the fourth acquisition of a Manhattan office property this year for Savanna, a firm with about $1 billion under management. There have been twists and turns to all of the firm's deals. For example, it purchased properties on Park Avenue South, Hanover Square and West 40th street after their owners had defaulted on the first mortgage.

The investment fund found the Broadway building compelling in part because of a different kind of wrinkle: the property had two separate owners. Chedward Realty Corp. owned the land while Statecourt Enterprises Inc. held a long-term ground lease that expires in 19 years.

As part of its purchase, Savanna reunited the lease and the so-called fee ownership. "Both sellers got more than they could have gotten individually, but we feel we got a good price in acquiring them together, given the level of rents in this neighborhood and recent comparable sales," says Mr. Bienstock.

Richard Baxter, vice chairman of the New York capital markets for Jones Lang Lasalle, says he thought Savanna got the property at a good price, in part because some potential buyers may have been put off by the separate ground lease.

Still, he added: "It's another sign of a resurgence in New York City prices," he said. With limited supply and scant speculative construction, prices in New York are likely to continue to rebound faster than most other property markets.

Write to Craig Karmin at craig.karmin@wsj.com[mailto:craig.karmin@wsj.com]

Dow Jones & Company, Inc.

Document WSJO000020101213e6cd0020b

Heard on the Street
Overheard: Sinking Feeling
188 words
11 December 2010
The Wall Street Journal
J
B18
English
(Copyright (c) 2010, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)

[Financial Analysis and Commentary]

Housing bulls mightn't want to dig too deeply into the data. Buried in the Federal Reserve's huge "flow of funds" report on Thursday was this bit of troubling news: The value of U.S. real estate sank by $698 billion in the third quarter. It was the first decline in almost two years, and it wiped out the gains of the prior five quarters. It also leaves the total value of U.S. real estate at about $18.3 trillion. That is a 26% drop from the 2006 peak and the lowest since 2003. With such a wealth hole left by the housing crash, no wonder the Fed is shoveling so furiously.

---

You can't keep private equity down. John Eydenburg, Deutsche Bank's head of financial sponsors, thinks 2011 will see the return of a $15 billion buyout. He says private equity has half-a-trillion dollars to invest: Leveraged 3-to-1, this "could buy something like 10% of the market cap of North America."

In Brief
108 words

The Wall Street Journal

Construction Spending

Rose in Latest Month

Spending on new construction rose in October for a second month in a row, due mainly to a pickup in residential construction, to a seasonally adjusted annual rate of $802.3 billion, up 0.7% from September, according to the Census Bureau. Spending on residential construction was up 2.4% in October to an annual rate of $240.3 billion but was down 8% from a year earlier. Nonresidential spending fell 0.1% in October from September to $562.0 billion and was off 9.9% from October 2009.

Economy
Jobless Claims Rise, Housing Perks Up
By Sudeep Reddy
408 words
3 December 2010
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2010 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.

New jobless claims climbed last week, damping hopes for a quickening improvement in the labor market. But a sharp gain in an indicator of future home sales offered a positive sign for the beleaguered housing market.

Initial claims for unemployment compensation rose 26,000 to a seasonally adjusted 436,000 in the week ended Nov. 27, the Labor Department said Thursday. The latest jump erased most of the prior week's substantial decline, a move that had suggested a rapid turn toward stronger employment.

Still, jobless claims are making slow progress toward healthier levels. The four-week moving average of claims, a measure used to smooth weekly volatility, stood at 431,000, the lowest since August 2008. Initial claims were close to 300,000 in the months before the recession started in late 2007.

Continuing claims, drawn by unemployed individuals for more than a week, climbed 53,000 to a seasonally adjusted 4.27 million in the week ended Nov. 20. The figure could drop later this month after an extension of unemployment benefits—to as long as 99 weeks—expired this week. Some two million people could lose their benefits by year's end unless Congress passes a new extension.

Pending Home Sales Increase

Meanwhile, a housing-industry index that tracks pending sales of existing homes gained 10.4% to 89.3 in October from a month earlier.

The National Association of Realtors index, which tracks home contracts that have been signed but not closed, indicates transactions coming in the following two months. It suggests a jump in overall existing home sales at least for November.

The October increase followed a 1.8% drop in September. Coming in a month in which some banks halted foreclosure sales, the gain offered hope that troubles with foreclosures wouldn't derail the overall market.

Still, the index remained more than 20% below its October 2009 mark. And its latest boost only brought it back to the level seen before a federal tax credit for home buyers drew buyers into the market and boosted sales.

"In absolute terms these remain very depressed levels, and there is considerably more territory to cover in order to call the move anything more than a rebound from a post-tax credit air pocket," said Joshua Shapiro, U.S. chief economist at MFR Inc., a consultancy.

Dow Jones & Company, Inc.

U.S. News: New jobless claims rose last week
By Sudeep Reddy
480 words
3 December 2010
The Wall Street Journal Europe
WSJE
8
English
(Copyright (c) 2010, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)

New jobless claims climbed last week, damping hopes for a quickening improvement in the labor market. But a sharp gain in an indicator of future home sales offered a positive sign for the beleaguered housing market.

Initial claims for unemployment compensation rose 26,000 to a seasonally adjusted 436,000 in the week ended Nov. 27, the Labor Department said Thursday. The latest jump erased most of the prior week's substantial decline, a move that had suggested a rapid turn toward stronger employment.

Still, jobless claims are making slow progress toward healthier levels. The four-week moving average of claims, a measure used to smooth weekly volatility, stood at 431,000, the lowest since August 2008. Initial claims were close to 300,000 in the months before the recession started in late 2007.

Continuing claims, drawn by unemployed individuals for more than a week, climbed 53,000 to a seasonally adjusted 4.27 million in the week ended Nov. 20. The figure could drop later this month after an extension of unemployment benefits -- to as long as 99 weeks -- expired this week. As a result, some two million people could lose their benefits by year's end unless Congress passes a new extension.

Meanwhile, a housing-industry index that tracks pending sales of existing homes gained 10.4% to 89.3 in October from a month earlier.

The National Association of Realtors index, which tracks home contracts that have been signed but not closed, indicates transactions coming in the following two months. It suggests a jump in overall existing home sales at least for November.

The October increase followed a 1.8% drop in September. Coming in a month in which some banks halted foreclosure sales, the gain offered hope that troubles with foreclosures wouldn't derail the overall market.

Still, the index remained more than 20% below its October 2009 mark. And its latest boost only brought it back to the level seen before a federal tax credit for home buyers drew buyers into the market and boosted sales.

"In absolute terms these remain very depressed levels, and there is considerably more territory to cover in order to call the move anything more than a rebound from a post-tax credit air pocket," said Joshua Shapiro, U.S. chief economist at MFR Inc., a consultancy.

Home prices in the U.S. are falling, which, along with a weak economy, hurts demand. Home prices nationally were down 2% in the third quarter compared with the second quarter, according to the S&P Case-Shiller national price index released this week.

While lower prices mean more people can afford homes, a downward trend in prices discourages some would-be buyers as they opt to wait for a better deal.

Commercial Real Estate
Risky Wager Pays Off; CB Richard Ellis Rallies With Its Gamble on Outsourcing
By A.D. Pruitt
837 words
1 December 2010
21:39
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2010 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.

(See Correction & Amplification below[U4015714970201LG] .)

CB Richard Ellis Group Inc.'s $2.2 billion acquisition of Trammell Crow near the top of the boom saddled the real-estate firm with so much debt, some worried during the depths of the downturn that the company would topple.

But now that CBRE has survived that near-death experience, the deal is looking smarter. By buying Trammell Crow, CBRE became the world's leading provider of services to Fortune 500 companies that own or lease property but want others to lease and manage it.

To cut expenses during the downturn, a growing number of corporations are outsourcing such duties, providing CBRE with a growing business at a time when uncertainty continues to grip the commercial real-estate industry.

Indeed, CBRE's outsourcing business has accounted for 40% of CBRE's global revenue this year, compared with 15% in 2006. In the first nine months of 2010, the unit raked in a record $1.3 billion in revenue. New clients include American International Group Inc., NYSE Euronext and United Technologies Corp. in Europe.

"The Crow acquisition gave us a tremendous buffer going into this unprecedented downturn and...was hugely helpful to us to maintaining profitability," said Brett White, chief executive of CBRE in an interview.

Competitors that don't rely heavily on providing outsourced services haven't fared as well. Shares of Grubb & Ellis Co. are trading at roughly $1.15 a share as its investment-management business struggles. That service accounts for roughly 50% of the company's earnings before interest, taxes, depreciation and amortization, said William Marks, an analyst at JMP Securities.

Cushman & Wakefield, a unit of Exor SpA, a public Italian company, is having a rough year even though its fortunes are improving. The company suffered an operating loss of $10.9 million during the first nine months of the year, compared with a loss of $93.8 million during the same period in 2009.

CBRE's stock, meanwhile, has soared about 40% since the beginning of the year, to more than $19 a share.

The outlook was much more dire in March 2009, when the company was grappling with insolvency concerns. Part of the problem was the 2006 acquisition of Trammell Crow, which added roughly $1.5 billion of debt to the company's balance sheet.

In late 2007, a year after the acquisition, the company opted to borrow to finance a $635 million share repurchase, at $22 a share. That was before its stock fell to $2.36, an all-time low, in March 2009. And in 2008 and 2009, the company had to raise about $650 million by selling equity at a low price, diluting shareholders.

But CBRE survived those dark days, partly thanks to its outsourcing business. The outsourcing services provided a solid revenue stream during most of last year as the company's traditional businesses of brokering sales and leasing transactions suffered because of the slow economy.

"Trammell gave [CBRE] a huge outsourcing platform that has only grown since the peak," said Mr. Marks.

While big corporations were avoiding making big leasing decisions, they also were cutting costs by hiring real-estate companies to run their buildings and manage their space needs. AIG's decision to hire CBRE last month will help the company "realize significant efficiencies and cost savings in our business operations world-wide," said Joe Barile, AIG senior vice president of global corporate real estate and client services, in a written statement.

CBRE's biggest competitor in outsourcing is Jones Lang LaSalle Inc., a Chicago-based real-estate company that has been in the business since the late 1980s. It too has benefited in recent years from having the business as a buffer against the strongest blows of the downturn. Jones Lang's share price has risen by about 30% since the beginning of the year.

"We continue to see a very strong acceleration in the number of [requests for proposals] for outsourcing," said Chris Browne, director of Jones Lang's corporate-solutions business. Mr. Browne says there has been double-digit growth in the firm's outsourcing business since 2006.

In the meantime, other firms are trying to grab a bigger piece of the outsourcing pie.

Grubb & Ellis spokeswoman Janice McDill said the company is looking to expand its outsourcing operations, focusing on middle-market companies.

John Santora, chief executive of Cushman & Wakefield's outsourcing group, says it plans to "aggressively" increase its revenue over the next five years. Currently the group makes up about 20% of the company's revenue.

"Recurring revenue helps you in down markets," Mr. Santora said.

Correction & Amplification

CB Richard Ellis Group Inc. is the property manager for the space that McGraw-Hill Cos. occupies in the building that bears McGraw-Hill's name. A photo caption that previously accompanied this article incorrectly said it managed the entire building.

Dow Jones & Company, Inc.

Real Estate
Morgan Stanley Sets Its Sights Lower
By Anton Troianovski
1034 words
1 December 2010
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2010 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.

During the boom years, Morgan Stanley's vaunted real-estate investing unit bought trophy office buildings in San Francisco, a Boston development project, and glittering lodging properties like Hawaii's Grand Wailea resort.

These days, the Wall Street giant is scouring the fringes of the property market in the hunt for high returns. One of its recent deals: a half-empty 60,000-square-foot shopping center in California's Inland Empire that houses a Fresh & Easy Neighborhood Market.

That contrast tells the story of a real-estate investment powerhouse cautiously trying to rebuild its reputation after two years of recording some of the worst losses in the history of the business. As one of Wall Street's biggest commercial-real-estate investors during the boom, the bank raised billions of dollars from pension funds and other big investors and went on a global buying binge. The recession stuck Morgan Stanley with severe losses—such as an $8.8 billion fund that about a year ago was projected to lose $5.4 billion—although some market improvements have led to write-ups in the firm's real-estate portfolio.

Morgan Stanley's most recent U.S. activity sheds light on how it is again searching for high-return opportunities.

In the U.S., Morgan Stanley's unit that seeks out "opportunistic," high-return real-estate investments has been driven toward overlooked and more volatile markets, buying, for example high-vacancy office buildings in Phoenix and Southern California. Instead of relying on borrowed money to boost returns, the firm is hoping to increase value by bringing deeply distressed properties back to health, a risky bet in an uncertain economy and one that is hard to execute in bite-size chunks on a multibillion-dollar scale.

Gregory Fleming, Morgan Stanley's president of investment management, said at a conference last month that the real-estate group was focused both on maximizing investor returns in existing funds as well as "putting the money to work that we have in the new vehicles in an environment where we think we can find attractive returns—always on a disciplined basis."

The firm's latest flagship opportunity fund, the $4.7 billion Morgan Stanley Real Estate Fund VII Global, or Msref VII, recently partnered with Lincoln Property Co. to pay $23 million in cash for a new, empty office building in the Los Angeles suburb of Glendale. That is less than half what the building cost to build, according to Lincoln executive David Binswanger, who voiced confidence he would be able to fill the building as local tenants move out of lower-quality office space nearby. But Glendale is one of Southern California's trickiest markets, with about one-fourth of the area's high-end office space sitting vacant.

Msref VII also participated in a deal for $155 million in real-estate loans acquired at a discount from Hawaii-based Central Pacific Bank. "Together, we're looking at more distressed portfolios," Jeffrey Dritley, managing partner of Msref's partner in the deal, Kearny Real Estate Co., said of Morgan Stanley. The Central Pacific Bank portfolio included Margarita Center, the 60,000-square-foot shopping center in Murrieta, Calif.

The fund has also done deals overseas, including 23 retail properties in Germany and four office buildings in Japan. Msref's joint venture in Germany says it plans to invest several hundred million euros in German retail real estate.

But in the U.S., Msref's recent deals have mostly been far smaller, seeking out markets avoided by the legions of real-estate investors who have been willing to pay top dollar for property in more stable markets like Washington, D.C.

"Contrary to conventional wisdom, we think the less-favorable, less-core markets can outperform in recoveries," Lauren Hochfelder Silverman, a Morgan Stanley executive director, said at a recent real-estate conference.

To avoid repeating past mistakes, Morgan Stanley has instituted new safeguards, including more internal procedures that have to be followed before investments are approved and chief financial officers assigned to each fund, people close to the firm's real-estate group say. Industry veterans John Klopp and Olivier de Poulpiquet were appointed to co-head the real-estate group in September.

But Morgan Stanley's more patient approach raises questions of its own—including whether the firm will be able to invest all the cash it has raised from investors. So far, Morgan Stanley has spent only about 20% of the $4.7 billion in commitments to Msref VII, a person familiar with the matter said. The firm is more than halfway through the fund's four-year "investment period" and faces a mid-2012 deadline after which it will no longer be able to call additional commitments. The slow start is frustrating some investors eager to see their cash deployed.

"That kind of makes us feel like we're sitting on the sidelines and our hands are tied," says Joaquin Lujan, real-estate portfolio manager for the New Mexico Public Employees Retirement Association, an investor in the Morgan Stanley fund.

Morgan Stanley has reason to be cautious. In the last two years, its investments in many real-estate deals—from stakes in U.S. developers to European hotels—have been wiped out. A disastrous $6.5 billion buyout of Crescent Real Estate Equities Co. led to $951 million in write-downs and other losses for the firm. The California State Teachers' Retirement System reported losses of more than 80% in Morgan Stanley's $8.8 billion international real-estate fund as of March 31—but an increase of about 10% in the first quarter of this year, according to the most recent data reported by Calstrs. The low-key approach shows up in other ways, too.

In 2007 alone, Morgan Stanley's real-estate unit published at least 30 press releases, many of them announcing big new acquisitions from St. Petersburg to Tokyo. Since March 12, 2008, the firm's Web real-estate website lists just one, a January 2010 announcement of a management change.

Write to Anton Troianovski at anton.troianovski@wsj.com[mailto:anton.troianovski@wsj.com]

Dow Jones & Company, Inc.

U.S. News: Slump in housing prices deepens
By Conor Dougherty and Matt Jarzemsky
392 words
1 December 2010
The Wall Street Journal Europe
WSJE
8
English
(Copyright (c) 2010, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)

The U.S. housing market is showing signs of falling deeper into a slump that could weigh on the nation's economic recovery.

Home prices nationwide were down 1.5% in the third quarter compared with a year ago, according to the S&P/Case-Shiller home-price index released Tuesday. The drop reflects the sharp fall in home sales after government home-buying tax credits expired earlier this year. Prices fell even more, by 2%, in the third quarter compared with the second quarter.

Case-Shiller's composite index for 20 major U.S. cities fell 0.7% in September, compared with August, not adjusted for seasonal variations. The biggest decline was in Cleveland, where prices fell 3% in September, compared with August. Minneapolis, Portland, Ore., and Phoenix were also big losers, with monthly price declines between 2.1% and 1.5%.

The best showing was in Washington, D.C., where prices increased 0.3% in September from August.

Despite an improving job market and growth in consumer spending, home prices and sales have stayed consistently depressed through the economy's year-old recovery. The expiration of federal tax credits for home buyers, problems in foreclosure processing and an unemployment rate still at 9.6% have kept buyers at bay and prompted sellers to cut prices.

Those drawbacks, combined with a large stock of unsold homes and distressed sales that hold down prices, have left new-home sales and construction near historic lows.

"The housing market is stuck at the bottom, and we've been stuck there for months," said Patrick Newport, an economist at IHS Global Insight.

A separate report Tuesday showed that consumers are feeling a bit better about the economy in spite of the woeful housing market. The Conference Board, a private research group, said its index of consumer confidence increased to 54.1 in November from 49.9 in October. While confidence remains low by historical standards, the November reading was the highest since June.

Consumers were more optimistic about business conditions in the near future. Those anticipating improving conditions in the next six months rose to 16.7% from 15.8%, while those expecting worsening conditions declined to 12.1% from 14.4%.
